By the Dominated Convergence Theorem, we can interchange the limit and the integral: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \int_{[0,1]} f(x) e^{-nx} \, dx = \int_{[0,1]} \lim_{n \to \infty} f(x) e^{-nx} \, dx = \int_{[0,1]} 0 \, dx = 0. \] Thus, the limit is \(\boxed{0}\).

Here, the exponential e^{-nx} is not oscillatory, but decaying. However, the principle that the integrand becomes highly oscillatory or decays to zero except on a set of measure zero might lead the integral to go to zero. But in this case, since it's a real exponential decay, not oscillations, the mechanism is different. In Fourier, the oscillations cause cancellation, but here, the decay causes the integrand to be small except near zero, but near zero, the integral can be made small due to absolute continuity. But regardless of the interpretation, the conclusion is the same. Therefore, the answer should be that by the Dominated Convergence Theorem, the integral tends to zero. **Final Answer** \boxed{0} </think> To show that \( \lim_{n \to \infty} (f, \chi_n) = 0 \) where \( (f, \chi_n) = \int_{[0,1]} f(x) e^{-nx} \, dx \) and \( f \in L^1([0,1]) \), we can use the Dominated Convergence Theorem (DCT). 1. **Pointwise Convergence**: For each \( x \in (0,1] \), \( e^{-nx} \to 0 \) as \( n \to \infty \). At \( x = 0 \), \( e^{-n \cdot 0} = 1 \), but the set \(\{0\}\) has measure zero. Therefore, \( e^{-nx} \to 0 \) almost everywhere on \([0,1]\). 2. **Dominating Function**: The absolute value of the integrand \( |f(x) e^{-nx}| \) is bounded by \( |f(x)| \), which is integrable since \( f \in L^1([0,1]) \). By the Dominated Convergence Theorem, we can interchange the limit and the integral: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \int_{[0,1]} f(x) e^{-nx} \, dx = \int_{[0,1]} \lim_{n \to \infty} f(x) e^{-nx} \, dx = \int_{[0,1]} 0 \, dx = 0. \] Thus, the limit is \(\boxed{0}\).

Can we deduce that the induced map on the first homology group with coefficients in \( \mathbb{Z}_3 \), \( H_1(f, \mathbb{Z}_3): H_1(X, \mathbb{Z}_3) \to H_1(X, \mathbb{Z}_3) \), is also an isomorphism? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

The key point is that the induced map on \( \pi_1 \) is an isomorphism, which implies the induced map on \( H_1 \) is an isomorphism, regardless of whether \( \pi_1 \) was abelian or not. The non-abelianness of \( \pi_1(X) \) might just be a hint that the abelianization is non-trivial, but even if \( \pi_1(X) \) were abelian, the same conclusion would hold. Therefore, unless there's some subtlety I'm missing here, the answer should be yes, \( H_1(f; \mathbb{Z}_3) \) is also an isomorphism. Wait, but let me check one more angle. Suppose \( \pi_1(f) \) is an isomorphism, so the induced map on \( H_1(\mathbb{Z}) \) is an isomorphism. Therefore, the induced map on \( H_1(\mathbb{Z}) \otimes \mathbb{Z}_3 \) is an isomorphism. But by universal coefficients, \( H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}_3) \cong H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}) \otimes \mathbb{Z}_3 \oplus \text{Tor}(H_0(X; \mathbb{Z}), \mathbb{Z}_3) \). But \( H_0(X; \mathbb{Z}) \) is free abelian, so Tor vanishes, hence \( H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}_3) \cong H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}) \otimes \mathbb{Z}_3 \). So, the map \( H_1(f; \mathbb{Z}_3) \) is isomorphic to \( H_1(f; \mathbb{Z}) \otimes \text{id}_{\mathbb{Z}_3} \). Since \( H_1(f; \mathbb{Z}) \) is an isomorphism, tensoring with \( \mathbb{Z}_3 \) gives an isomorphism. Therefore, \( H_1(f; \mathbb{Z}_3) \) is indeed an isomorphism. Therefore, the answer is yes. But wait, hold on. Let me think again. Suppose the fundamental group is non-abelian, so its abelianization \( H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}) \) is a quotient. If the induced map on \( \pi_1 \) is an isomorphism, then it must preserve the commutator subgroup, so the induced map on the quotient \( H_1 \) is an isomorphism. Then, as above, tensoring with \( \mathbb{Z}_3 \) preserves the isomorphism. Therefore, regardless of the non-abelianness, the conclusion holds. Therefore, the answer should be yes. But let me check if there is a possible mistake in assuming that tensoring an isomorphism with \( \mathbb{Z}_3 \) gives an isomorphism. If the original map \( H_1(f; \mathbb{Z}) \) is an isomorphism, then each component (free and torsion) is an isomorphism. Tensoring with \( \mathbb{Z}_3 \) would then either kill torsion components not divisible by 3 or preserve them if they are. However, since \( H_1(f; \mathbb{Z}) \) is an isomorphism, it must map torsion subgroups to themselves isomorphically. Therefore, if there is 3-torsion, it's preserved; if there is torsion not divisible by 3, then tensoring with \( \mathbb{Z}_3 \) annihilates them, but since the original map was an isomorphism on the entire \( H_1 \), the induced map on the remaining part (the free part tensored with \( \mathbb{Z}_3 \) and the 3-torsion) would still be an isomorphism. Therefore, regardless of the coefficients, as long as the original map on \( H_1(\mathbb{Z}) \) is an isomorphism, the induced map on \( H_1(\mathbb{Z}_3) \) is as well. Therefore, I think the answer is yes, we can deduce that \( H_1(f, \mathbb{Z}_3) \) is an isomorphism. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given a topological space \( X \) with a non-abelian fundamental group \( \pi_1(X) \) and a map \( f: X \to X \) such that the induced map \( \pi_1(f): \pi_1(X) \to \pi_1(X) \) is an isomorphism, we need to determine if the induced map \( H_1(f, \mathbb{Z}_3): H_1(X, \mathbb{Z}_3) \to H_1(X, \mathbb{Z}_3) \) is also an isomorphism. 1. **Fundamental Group and Homology**: The first homology group \( H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}) \) is the abelianization of \( \pi_1(X) \). Since \( \pi_1(f) \) is an isomorphism, it induces an isomorphism on the abelianization \( H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}) \). 2. **Coefficient Change**: The first homology group with coefficients in \( \mathbb{Z}_3 \), \( H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}_3) \), can be obtained using the universal coefficient theorem. This theorem states that \( H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}_3) \cong H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}) \otimes \mathbb{Z}_3 \oplus \text{Tor}(H_0(X; \mathbb{Z}), \mathbb{Z}_3) \). Since \( H_0(X; \mathbb{Z}) \) is free abelian, the Tor term vanishes, simplifying to \( H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}_3) \cong H_1(X; \mathbb{Z}) \otimes \mathbb{Z}_3 \). 3. **Induced Map on Homology**: The induced map \( H_1(f; \mathbb{Z}_3) \) is equivalent to \( H_1(f; \mathbb{Z}) \otimes \text{id}_{\mathbb{Z}_3} \). Since \( H_1(f; \mathbb{Z}) \) is an isomorphism, tensoring it with \( \mathbb{Z}_3 \) preserves the isomorphism. This is because tensoring an isomorphism with any module (here, \( \mathbb{Z}_3 \)) results in an isomorphism. Thus, the induced map \( H_1(f, \mathbb{Z}_3) \) is indeed an isomorphism. \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Let $a_n=6^{n}+8^{n}$. Determine the remainder upon dividing $a_ {83}$ by $49$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Since $\phi(49) = 42$ (see Euler's totient function), Euler's Totient Theorem tells us that $a^{42} \equiv 1 \pmod{49}$ where $\text{gcd}(a,49) = 1$. Thus $6^{83} + 8^{83} \equiv 6^{2(42)-1}+8^{2(42)-1}$ $\equiv 6^{-1} + 8^{-1} \equiv \frac{8+6}{48}$ $\equiv \frac{14}{-1}\equiv \boxed{35} \pmod{49}$.

Begin by adding the two equations: \begin{align*} 2x &= 52 \\ x &= 26 \end{align*}Now, subtract the two equations \begin{align*} 2y &= 28 \\ y &= 14 \end{align*}So then $x \cdot y = 26 \cdot 14 = \boxed{364}$.

Reference Answer: Let's start by rewriting this problem into equation form: \begin{align*} x + y &= 40, \\ x - y &= 12. \end{align*}We want to find $xy$, so let's find $x$ and $y$ separately. Begin by adding the two equations: \begin{align*} 2x &= 52 \\ x &= 26 \end{align*}Now, subtract the two equations \begin{align*} 2y &= 28 \\ y &= 14 \end{align*}So then $x \cdot y = 26 \cdot 14 = \boxed{364}$.
